{"architecture": "PARENT_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_pipeline.HoverMultiHopPipeline\nMETRIC_MODULE_PATH: langProBe.hover.hover_utils.discrete_retrieval_eval\n\n## Architecture Summary\n\n**Purpose**: This is a multi-hop document retrieval system for fact-checking claims using the HoVer (Hover-nlp) dataset. The system performs iterative retrieval across three hops to find supporting documents for veracity assessment of claims.\n\n**Key Modules**:\n- **HoverMultiHopPipeline** (hover_pipeline.py): Top-level pipeline wrapper that initializes the ColBERTv2 retrieval model and orchestrates the HoverMultiHop program\n- **HoverMultiHop** (hover_program.py): Core DSPy module implementing the 3-hop retrieval logic with query generation and summarization at each hop\n- **hoverBench** (hover_data.py): Dataset handler that loads and filters HoVer dataset to 3-hop examples, creating train/test splits\n- **discrete_retrieval_eval** (hover_utils.py): Evaluation metric that checks if all gold supporting document titles are retrieved (maximum 21 documents)\n\n**Data Flow**:\n1. Input claim \u2192 Hop 1: Direct retrieval (k=7 docs) + summarization\n2. Summary 1 \u2192 Hop 2: Generate new query via ChainOfThought, retrieve k=7 docs, summarize with context\n3. Summaries 1&2 \u2192 Hop 3: Generate refined query, retrieve final k=7 docs\n4. Output: Concatenated 21 documents (7 per hop) as retrieved_docs prediction\n\n**Metric**: discrete_retrieval_eval compares normalized gold document titles against retrieved document titles, returning True if all gold titles are found within the retrieved set (subset check)."}

Iteration 1: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop2.predict: Given input fields `claim` and `summary_1` (where `summary_1` is an evidence-based evaluation or factual assessment of the claim), generate a detailed `query` field designed to retrieve or verify supporting evidence for evaluating the claim. The `query` should be formulated as one or more effective, focused search queries or requests for specific authoritative information sources that can confirm or refute elements of the claim, considering the information and gaps noted in `summary_1`.

The goal is to identify precise factual points in the claim that need verification or further evidence, as well as the key entities, terms, dates, or relationships involved. Then formulate multiple targeted, structured search queries (or relevant information requests) that are likely to retrieve authoritative, trustworthy sources (e.g., official biographies, filmographies, academic citations, news articles, databases, or primary documents) addressing those points.

Instructions and best practices for creating the outputs:

1. **Analyze the claim carefully:** Break down the claim into component factual assertions—such as names, dates, roles, relationships, locations, affiliations, or quantitative data—that the summary confirms, denies, or leaves uncertain.

2. **Use the summary to identify information gaps:** Use `summary_1` to highlight what is already supported, contradicted, or unverified and the reasons why (e.g., missing data, ambiguity, or conflated concepts).

3. **Focus queries on verifying disputed or unsupported parts:** For each aspect of the claim that is not fully supported or contradicted by the summary, formulate explicit queries that could retrieve evidence to confirm or reject it.

4. **Include entity-specific details and contextual connections:** Incorporate proper names, dates, affiliations, and related terms from both the claim and summary to narrow search results to relevant, authoritative sources (e.g., official bios, film credits, event dates, institutional data).

5. **Apply logical search operators and structured phrasing:** Use combinations of keywords, Boolean operators (AND, OR), quotation marks for exact phrases, and synonyms to maximize precision and coverage.

6. **Generate multiple queries if necessary:** When multiple subclaims or facts require distinct verification, number and separate queries clearly, addressing each key factual element or claim component.

7. **Incorporate domain-specific knowledge when appropriate:** Use domain-specific terminology or concepts (e.g., "roman à clef" for literary terms, film titles with release years, recognized academic society names, recognized scientific or botanical taxonomies, official event naming conventions, recognized entities like sports team names or organizational acronyms).

8. **Request authoritative types of sources where helpful:** Direct searches toward sources typical for verifying the claim's domain—official websites, academic publications, filmographies (e.g. IMDb), government or organizational statistics, biographies, news archives, reputable databases.

9. **When claims are qualitative or comparative, seek quantitative or qualitative indicators:** For claims about popularity, relative size, or status, include queries to find measurable evidence such as attendance figures, ratings, sales numbers, rankings, demographic data, or widely accepted expert assessments.

10. **Clarify ambiguous terms if needed:** When the summary notes ambiguities or conflations (e.g., conflating two different deities), include queries that separately clarify those terms or entities.

11. **When claims rely on combined facts, create queries addressing the parts separately to confirm their validity and joint truthfulness.**

12. **Use natural language queries where appropriate for complex inquiries, but prefer concise keyword-driven queries for straightforward fact checks.**

Example structure:
- Numbered list of search queries, each addressing a specific fact or aspect of the claim.
- Queries framed as exact phrases in quotes, combined with key entity names, dates, or contextual indicators.
- Indication of type of sources intended to check (e.g., "site:imdb.com", "official biography," "government statistics," etc.) when relevant.

The output `query` field thus serves as a precise search plan or evidence request checklist tailored to verify or falsify the claim based on gaps and claims identified in the summary, guiding fact-checkers or automated systems toward relevant evidence.

Iteration 3: Proposed new text for program.adaptive_query_gen.predict: You are given a claim along with an analysis of information gaps that need to be filled to verify or refute the claim, plus optionally prior summaries of evidence availability. Your task is to generate targeted web search queries that can efficiently produce authoritative, relevant, and specific sources to close these identified gaps.

Your inputs will be:
- A factual claim involving one or more named entities, events, relationships, or data points.
- A gap analysis explicitly listing what key facts, identities, dates, associations, or source types are currently unknown or unconfirmed but necessary to verify the claim thoroughly.
- Optionally, shortened previous summaries of what evidence has been found or not.
  
Your output should include:

1. **Reasoning**: A concise explanation that synthesizes the gaps into concrete research needs, mapping from the abstract gaps to specific named entities, events, attributes, or key keywords that must appear in search results. It should clarify what kinds of sources are most appropriate (e.g., official databases, film credits, professional biographies, museum catalogs, governmental or academic websites, primary documents) depending on the domain and task.

2. **Queries**: Formulate one or more precise, well-structured web search queries that combine identified key phrases, names, date ranges, and domain/site filters to retrieve authoritative information. The queries should:
   - Reflect all distinct facts or pieces of information required to verify the claim, often as multiple queries if different domains or fact groups are involved.
   - Include exact phrases or names in quotes where relevant.
   - Use Boolean operators (`AND`, `OR`) and parentheses for clarity.
   - Apply site/domain targeting (`site:` operator) to prioritize databases, official pages, archives, or trusted media outlets specialized in the subject area.
   - Incorporate synonyms and variations of key terms (e.g., "birth date" OR "born") to capture different phrasings used in authoritative sources.
   - Include relevant date filters or years, if helpful, to narrow results effectively.
  
Additional domain-specific details learned from the examples:

- For **film and television claims**, target authoritative industry databases (IMDb, BFI, Variety, The Hollywood Reporter), official awards pages, studio or network press releases, and respected news sites. Focus on actor/director/writer credits, premiere dates, award nominations, and production roles.
- For **historical or biographical claims**, prioritize academic sources, museum or archive collections, genealogical databases, official vital records, national biographies, and reliable encyclopedias (Britannica, Oxford DNB).
- For **music-related claims**, use AllMusic, Discogs, official artist pages, music biographies, news outlets, and authoritative discographies.
- For **geographical/place naming claims**, consult USGS/GNIS databases, local history or historical society publications, obituary and cemetery records, and official government geographic name registries.
- For **pop culture or fandom-related claims**, consider official park or event sites, archived news articles, or official production documentation.
- For complex or multipart claims, separate queries targeting each fact or dimension should be provided to maximize coverage and precision.

General strategies:

- Disambiguate named entities by including context (e.g., full names, alternate spellings, birth years).
- Cross-reference connected entities or events explicitly in queries to ensure linking of facts.
- Use multiple site filters devoted to trusted information hubs relevant to the topic.
- Formulate queries to capture both primary sources (official data, direct credits, archival material) and high-quality secondary sources (reputable biographies, academic or news articles).
- Where multiple interpretations exist (e.g., ambiguous song titles, festivals, or people with similar names), include disambiguating keywords to ensure focus on the intended subject.

Your output format must always separate reasoning and the concrete query/queries, reflecting the strategy behind the searches and the actual search query text to be submitted to a search engine.

Iteration 5: Proposed new text for program.document_scorer.predict: You will be provided with a claim and one or more documents. Your task is to assess and score the relevance of each document in verifying the given claim. The relevance score should reflect how much the document helps confirm or refute the claim based on explicit factual content.

To do this, follow these guidelines:

1. **Understand the Claim Fully:** 
   - Identify all key elements, named entities (people, places, events, dates, works, organizations), numerical data (dates, populations, counts), and relationships stated in the claim.
   - Claims often contain complex or multi-part facts; ensure you parse each element carefully.

2. **Analyze Document Content:**
   - Check if the document mentions any key entities, facts, or relationships relevant to the claim.
   - Look for direct references or implications that support or contradict parts of the claim.
   - Note that a document can be relevant even if it only partially covers some elements of the claim, as long as it provides factual evidence about those elements.

3. **Relevance Scoring:**
   - Score should be a floating-point value typically between 0.0 (completely irrelevant) and 1.0 (highly relevant).
   - Assign a low score (close to 0) if the document:
     - Does not mention any key entities or facts from the claim.
     - Provides no factual information useful for verifying the claim.
   - Assign a moderate to high score (e.g., above 0.5 to 1.0) when the document:
     - Mentions multiple claim-specific entities/facts.
     - Provides information that can be used to support or refute the claim—including contextual or background info closely tied to claim elements.

4. **Reasoning Explanation:**
   - Clearly explain your thought process.
   - State which parts of the document relate to which aspects of the claim.
   - Specify what information is missing or present.
   - Explain why the document helps or does not help verify the claim.

5. **Domain-Specific Knowledge and Nuances:**
   - The claims and documents may involve specialized domain knowledge, e.g.:
     - Film and TV: directors, actors, films’ languages, roles, release dates.
     - Geography: population data, locations of towns/counties, airport codes.
     - Sports: player birthdates, match results.
     - Music: video directors, song titles.
     - History and Literature: opera characteristics, authorship
     - Science: Nobel laureates, scientific concepts, academic affiliations.
   - In many cases, exact matches of names, dates, or titles are necessary to establish relevance.
   - Be aware of potential minor name variants or title phrasing that might still signify the same entity.
   - Numeric or date figures must be linked precisely to entities or places mentioned in the claim.

6. **Partial Relevance and Missing Links:**
   - If the document provides information about some but not all key aspects of the claim, weigh its relevance according to the claim’s complexity and importance of matching facts.
   - Documents that refer to related but distinct entities or events (e.g., a different film or a different person) should generally be scored low or zero.

7. **Avoid Errors in Entity Matching:**
   - Confirm that entities mentioned in the document directly correspond to those in the claim.
   - Do not infer unrelated or broad connections where none are supported by the text.

Summary:  
Evaluate how strongly the document’s content helps verify factual elements of the claim, provide detailed reasoning for your scoring, and output a relevance score reflecting this analysis.

Output format:  
- A reasoning paragraph with detailed explanation.  
- A numeric relevance_score reflecting the relevance as explained above.

Iteration 7: Proposed new text for coverage_analyzer.predict: Task: Given a claim along with a set of retrieved documents relevant to that claim, your goal is to identify which entities, concepts, or specific factual pieces of information are missing from the retrieved documents that are necessary to fully verify (support or refute) the claim.

Input format:  
- Claim: A statement containing one or more factual assertions involving people, organizations, locations, works (films, songs, albums, books), products, historical events, dates, and so forth.  
- Retrieved Passages: A set of documents or excerpts retrieved as context related to the claim. Each passage varies in specificity and coverage, often containing entries on people, companies, works, events, or technical concepts related to the claim.

Detailed task description:  
1. **Comprehend the claim thoroughly**, noting all entities (e.g., persons, organizations, works), concepts (e.g., product categories, genres, historical contexts), and factual assertions (e.g., relationships, production facts, chronology).  
2. **Analyze the provided retrieved passages**, and determine whether each part of the claim is fully supported with explicit evidence or if some factual elements/relationships are missing or not clearly stated.  
3. **Identify missing entities or concepts**: These are specific facts or evidence not present in the retrieved passages but essential to verify the claim. Missing items can include:  
   - Explicit confirmation of relationships (e.g., which company produced a product; who signed a band; who owns a company).  
   - Clarification of ambiguous terms in the claim (e.g., “educational board” meaning specific institution/organization).  
   - Supporting data on attributes of entities (e.g., birthplaces, roles, dates, industry specialization).  
   - Precise connections between multiple entities (e.g., co-starring in the same film, familial relations, ownership stakes).  
   - Disambiguated names or corrections (e.g., author full names, alternate titles).  
   - Official or authoritative sources of critical facts such as company status, geographic locations, historical facts, or specific credits.  
4. **Incorporate domain-specific knowledge to understand specialized terminology or context** (e.g., knowledge of films and their casts, music industry terms, airline alliances and shareholding, military history terms, language classification, brand valuation concepts, computer microcontrollers).  
5. **Consider the potential ambiguity in the claim’s phrasing** and recommend clarifications as necessary.  
6. **Produce a detailed list of missing entities or concepts**, phrased as explicit information or sources that would be needed to fully verify the claim from the retrieved data.  
7. Do **not** assume facts absent in the documents but commonly known elsewhere; only rely on the given retrieved passages and identify missing information relative to these.  

Generalizable strategy:  
- Break down the claim into discrete factual elements.  
- Check if each element is explicitly or implicitly supported by one or more retrieved passages.  
- For unsupported or partially supported elements, identify precisely what specific fact, relationship, or authoritative source is required but absent.  
- Note ambiguity or inconsistencies in claim wording requiring clarification or additional context.  
- When multiple entities or concepts connect in the claim, verify their linkage explicitly in the passages; if absent, add their connecting evidence as missing.  
- Ensure to cover domain-specific nuances (like company ownership stakes, film cast credits, language classifications, historical timelines) as these often are crucial missing facts.  

Output: A comprehensive list enumerating all entities, concepts, or factual data missing from the retrieved passages that are essential to verifying the claim, including clarification needs or authoritative source citations that would resolve any ambiguity or gaps in evidence.

Iteration 9: Proposed new text for gap_query_generator.predict: Your task is to generate highly targeted, precise, and comprehensive search queries to retrieve authoritative and authoritative-leaning documents or web pages that fill all significant gaps identified in a coverage analysis for a given factual claim. The goal is to collect sources that enable verification or refutation of each claim element by addressing specific missing entities, ambiguous references, or under-supported facts.

The inputs will include:  
- A factual claim that may contain ambiguous, incomplete, or unsupported elements.  
- A detailed list of "missing_entities" or facts that must be confirmed or clarified to fully verify the claim.  

Your output must consist of two key parts:  
1. **Reasoning:** A clear, structured explanation of the distinct factual gaps and ambiguities in the claim based on the "missing_entities," and why each is needed to verify the claim. It should identify all critical evidentiary needs, the type(s) of documentation best suited to meet them (e.g., authoritative databases, official records, press releases, filmographies, bibliographies, corporate filings, government data, historical archives), and any necessary disambiguation steps or cross-referencing. This reasoning should guide retrieval prioritization and justify the design of the queries.  

2. **Gap Query:** Formulate one or more complex, carefully crafted boolean search queries or sets of focused search phrases intended to run on general web search engines or specialized domain sites (e.g., imdb.com, wikipedia.org, government archives, corporate sites, academic repositories). The queries should be:  
- Explicit in enumerating all relevant key terms, names, dates, and synonyms needed to match the full scope of missing facts.  
- Include site-restrictions or domain filters to focus results on authoritative and trustworthy sources recognized for the domain of knowledge involved.  
- Cover possible alternative titles, variants, or ambiguous references present in the claim (e.g., multiple film versions, alternate spellings, institution name variants).  
- Designed to elicit documents containing critical direct evidence such as official cast lists, filmographies, award nomination details, corporate shareholder records, historical mission logs, or verified biographies.  
- Optionally, if the retrieval system does not support complex boolean or site grouping syntax, queries should be modularized or broken into simpler parts that collectively address the gaps.  

**General strategy to apply:**  
- Identify all problematic ambiguities and missing fact sets from the input.  
- For each missing entity or ambiguous concept, determine what primary or high-quality secondary sources can confirm or disprove it (e.g., IMDb for film/cast, official corporate reports for shareholder data, government registries for institutional facts, reputable news or scholarly databases for award or biographical data).  
- Include synonyms, alternative titles, and key related entities in the query to maximize recall.  
- Use site filters or domain keywords to constrain to authoritative sources.  
- Where applicable, combine multiple related information needs into composite queries using OR/AND logic to minimize query runs.  
- Emphasize sourcing direct documentary evidence (e.g., award ceremony details, official film credits, mission logs, corporate filings, university program listings) over indirect or anecdotal references.  
- Where the claim involves temporal or status ambiguities (e.g., current vs historical ownership), queries should attempt to capture timeline or date information to resolve such issues.  

Your output produces the chain of reasoning demonstrating your understanding of the verification gaps and a gap-filling retrieval query tailored to maximize the precision and completeness of evidence retrieved, enabling subsequent detailed claim verification or factual adjudication.

Iteration 11: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: Given the input fields `claim`, `summary_1`, and `summary_2`, produce the field `query`. 

**Task objective:**  
You are tasked with generating targeted, effective search queries that can be used to gather authoritative evidence to verify, refute, or clarify the factual accuracy of the given `claim` based on the information (or lack thereof) in the two provided summaries.  

**Input details:**  
- `claim`: A factual statement or assertion (sometimes complex or multi-part) whose truthfulness is to be evaluated.  
- `summary_1` and `summary_2`: Two short textual evidence summaries derived from provided source passages. They indicate whether the information supports, contradicts, or provides insufficient evidence regarding the claim. Each summary may have different or overlapping assessments and facts noted from the source material.

**Output:**  
- `query`: One or multiple precisely formulated search queries or a set of targeted requests designed to find external, reliable, and verifiable information required to resolve ambiguities or missing information highlighted by the summaries to conclusively evaluate the claim.

**Key domain-specific details and strategies to incorporate:**  

1. **Identify gaps and conflicts in evidence**  
   - Analyze both summaries to detect what information is supported, contradicted, or missing.  
   - Focus queries on the exact unresolved aspects, e.g., specific factual details, dates, personnel, relationships, roles, or attributes absent or unclear from the summaries.
   - When summaries conflict, queries should aim to acquire definitive evidence to resolve the contradiction.

2. **Include all relevant entities and facts from the claim and summaries**  
   - Extract key named entities (persons, organizations, places, works, events) related to the claim.  
   - Include alternative spellings or related terms if misspellings or ambiguous naming occurs in the claim or summaries.  
   - Also include indirect references or clarifications suggested by the summaries (e.g., misspelled names, alternative terms, or relevant affiliated entities).

3. **Design queries for authoritative and reliable sources**  
   - Prefer keywords that will surface authoritative websites: sites ending in `.edu`, `.gov`, `.org` for official or academic sources when factual historical, scientific, or institutional verification is needed.  
   - Use recognized domain-specific repositories such as IMDb, Wikipedia, Playbill, Discogs, Tony Awards database, or other domain-specific databases reflecting the field of the claim (e.g., music, film, academia, sport).  
   - Include keywords like "biography," "filmography," "cast list," "release date," "award," "history," "formation year," "tenure," "credits," "roles," or similar terms relevant to the specific domain/fact type.

4. **When the claim is multi-part or contains multiple factual assertions, generate queries that address each part separately or collectively but clearly**  
   - Enumerate search queries when appropriate, isolating each factual aspect to avoid missing details.  
   - If applicable, indicate the need for citations, passage quotes, or source links that explicitly confirm or refute details.

5. **Clarify ambiguous or complex claim components**  
   - When claims mention persons or events without sufficient disambiguation in summaries, include queries that clarify identities (e.g., confirm which "Tom Johnston" is referenced).  
   - When claim details contradict common knowledge or have temporal or locational ambiguities, tailor queries to confirm specifics (e.g., formation dates, event chronology).

6. **Use Boolean operators and phrase searching**  
   - Use quotes around key phrases and names for exact matches.  
   - Combine search terms with Boolean operators (`AND`, `OR`) to refine search focus.  
   - Incorporate proximate searches or site restrictions if needed for precision.

7. **If the claim references awards or categories (such as "Best Score and Best Orchestrations"), clarify that they are awards, not work titles, and query accordingly.**

**Summary:**  
The generated `query` serves as a precise, comprehensive search strategy designed to gather decisive evidence from authoritative sources addressing the factual gaps and contradictions identified between the claim and its two supporting summaries. It should be domain-aware and leveraged with knowledge of niche sources and terminology relevant to the claim’s subject matter. The overall goal is to optimize external information retrieval that enables confident factual verification or falsification of the claim.

Iteration 13: Proposed new text for program.create_query_hop3.predict: Task Description:

You will be given three text fields as input:  
- `claim`: A factual or composite factual claim that may combine multiple assertions or entities.   
- `summary_1` and `summary_2`: Two different summaries or assessments of the evidence related to the `claim`. These summarize the supporting or refuting evidence found (or missing) in the relevant passages/documents.

Your task is to produce a `query` output field that contains a set of specific, clear, and targeted information retrieval queries or search instructions designed to obtain authoritative, verifiable evidence necessary to verify, refute, or further clarify the factual components of the `claim`. The queries should be:

1. **Factually and conceptually derived:**  
   Analyze the `claim` and both summaries to identify points of conflict, ambiguity, missing evidence, or key entities and facts referenced. Focus on the specific pieces of information that are needed to fully verify or refute the `claim`.  

2. **Comprehensive and precise:**   
   Break down the claim into discrete facts or assertions. For each fact for which evidence is incomplete or conflicting in the summaries, formulate queries that uniquely target these facts and their associated entities (people, works, places, dates, roles, relationships, events, or numbers).  

3. **Domain-aware and context-sensitive:**  
   - Where names of people, works (films, songs, books), organizations, or locations are mentioned, queries should specify these clearly, often including context words like "biography," "filmography," "discography," "cast," "director," "release date," "award," "occupation," "producer," "composer," "birth date," "nationality," etc.  
   - Use known authoritative sources implicitly or explicitly (e.g., IMDb, Wikipedia, official sites, music databases, encyclopedias, Wikidata) when feasible.  
   - Include search terms or queries to confirm relationships or attributes (e.g., "director of X," "composer of Y," "award won by Z," "birth year of A").  

4. **Structured and actionable:**  
   Present the queries as numbered or bulleted points for clarity, sometimes separating them into sub-queries or specifying search terms within quotation marks or boolean syntax where helpful. Queries may include site-specific searches or suggest exact phrases likely to retrieve precise evidence.  

5. **Balance thoroughness and focus:**  
   Avoid overly broad or vague queries; instead, provide targeted, fact-specific queries that will efficiently locate the key supporting or refuting evidence needed.

6. **If applicable, request direct citations or URLs:**   
   When appropriate, instruct to obtain direct evidence including URLs or bibliographic citations for verification.

Generalizable strategy demonstrated in examples:  
- Start by identifying the minimal set of unresolved factual points—from the conflict or insufficiency between `summary_1` and `summary_2` or from missing data noted in summaries.  
- Convert those points into distinct queries referencing exact entities or facts to be verified.  
- Include any necessary disambiguation or context that will help isolate the correct information (e.g., film release years, director names, song titles plus artist name).  
- Use authoritative sources or knowledge bases as suggested targets.  
- Format the output in a clear, retrievable format listing search queries or instructions.

Summary of domain-specific knowledge embedded in instructions:  
- Understanding of film metadata: director, cast, release year, production details.  
- Recognition of musical works, composers, covers, sales figures.  
- Awareness of biographical data: birth dates, nationalities, occupations/professions.  
- Knowledge of organizational structures: leagues, sponsorships, membership.  
- Historical and cultural references tied to art installations or monuments.  
- Ability to formulate queries comparing or verifying relationships between persons, works, or entities.  
- Use of multiple layers of supporting facts extracted from the summaries to pinpoint relevant evidence needs.  

Use these instructions as a guideline for converting any `claim` and associated evidence summaries into actionable and pinpointed search queries designed to yield reliable confirmatory or refutatory evidence for claim verification.

[AGENT] Change request (full): Implement a **"Iterative Query Decomposition with Bridge Entity Recognition"** architecture in `langProBe/hover/hover_pipeline.py:HoverMultiHopPipeline.forward()`:

1. **Stage 1 - Claim Decomposition**: Create a new `ClaimDecomposer` signature and module using `dspy.ChainOfThought` that analyzes the claim and breaks it into 2-3 sequential sub-questions representing the logical hops needed (e.g., "Who starred in Film X?" → "What other films did that person star in?"). Each sub-question should explicitly identify what bridge entities connect the hops.

2. **Stage 2 - Sequential Bridge Retrieval**: For each decomposed sub-question:
   - Create a `BridgeEntityExtractor` signature that extracts the specific entity connecting to the next hop (e.g., actor name, company name, location)
   - Retrieve k=50 documents using the sub-question as query
   - Extract the bridge entity from retrieved documents using the new extractor module
   - Use the bridge entity to formulate the next sub-question's query

3. **Stage 3 - Intermediate Representation Reranking**: Following research showing intermediate layer representations work better for multi-hop retrieval, create a `MultiHopRelevanceScorer` signature that scores documents based on: (a) direct relevance to sub-question, (b) presence of bridge entities, and (c) how documents connect across reasoning hops. Use this to select top 7-10 documents per hop.

4. **Stage 4 - Final Fusion**: Combine all documents from the 2-3 hops (total retrieval budget: 2-3 searches as per constraint), deduplicate, and apply a final `CrossHopConnectivityRanker` that scores documents based on how well they form a complete reasoning chain, selecting the final 21 documents.

This architecture explicitly models the multi-step reasoning paths required for multi-hop retrieval, inspired by the DeCoR framework and research on bridge entity reasoning.
